  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer13523136510936534344">“One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking—it was coming from her parents’ bedroom.”<br/><br/>This sentence opens John Irving’s ninth novel, A Widow for One Year, a story of a family marked by tragedy. Ruth Cole is a complex, often self-contradictory character—a “difficult” woman. By no means is she convent</span>
  <span id="freeText13523136510936534344" style="display:none">“One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking—it was coming from her parents’ bedroom.”<br /><br />This sentence opens John Irving’s ninth novel, A Widow for One Year, a story of a family marked by tragedy. Ruth Cole is a complex, often self-contradictory character—a “difficult” woman. By no means is she conventionally “nice,” but she will never be forgotten.<br /><br />Ruth’s story is told in three parts, each focusing on a critical time in her life. When we first meet her—on Long Island, in the summer of 1958—Ruth is only four.<br /><br />The second window into Ruth’s life opens on the fall of 1990, when she is an unmarried woman whose personal life is not nearly as successful as her literary career. She distrusts her judgment in men, for good reason.<br /><br />A Widow for One Year closes in the autumn of 1995, when Ruth Cole is a forty-one-year-old widow and mother. She’s about to fall in love for the first time.<br /><br />Richly comic, as well as deeply disturbing, A Widow for One Year is a multilayered love story of astonishing emotional force. Both ribald and erotic, it is also a brilliant novel about the passage of time and the relentlessness of grief.<br />Source: john-irving.com</span>
  <a data-text-id="13523136510936534344" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">no one</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3022849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10060593612596913724">I hated this book. John Iriving's inability to write women characters was a huge problem in this book since it has a female protagonist. I didn't care about her at all and I wasn't that intrigued by the story either. I generally like John Irving's writing style, but it didn't make any difference to me with this book because I didn't like one single character.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">horny bored housewives</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8515110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6132819334893913314">OK here's my final word on John Irving, because I will probably never read anything else he's written (though I've heard The World According to Garp is his best.) His characters are real and they were JUST ENOUGH to keep me going each of the twenty times I nearly stopped reading this novel. The plot is a rambling patchwork in which we never, ever, forget the writer sitting at his typewriter, searching for something to say. When he finds it, he riffs on it till it dies, and then searches for some</span>
  <span id="freeText6132819334893913314" style="display:none">OK here's my final word on John Irving, because I will probably never read anything else he's written (though I've heard The World According to Garp is his best.) His characters are real and they were JUST ENOUGH to keep me going each of the twenty times I nearly stopped reading this novel. The plot is a rambling patchwork in which we never, ever, forget the writer sitting at his typewriter, searching for something to say. When he finds it, he riffs on it till it dies, and then searches for something else. I felt sorry for his characters, having to submit to such an unbelievable series of twists and turns.</span>

<div id="review_1768892637" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1768892637" />

This book otherwise not only paints a vivid, realistic portrait of grief, but also love and nostalgia.</span>

<div id="review_10471953" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10471953" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/686677-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1197748277p2/686677.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10471953?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/686677-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10471953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10131275221021914336">John Irving has yet again created a whole world between the covers of a novel. Characters grow old with the reader, experience lust and loss, love and life. The thoughtfulness of his every detail and the concise placement of every word create a landscape more vivid than reality<br/>One of the interesting topics of conversation in A Widow for One Year involves the main character’s attitude towards autobiographical fiction. Irving’s protagonist, world-famous author Ruth Cole, gives one hope that the po</span>
  <span id="freeText10131275221021914336" style="display:none">John Irving has yet again created a whole world between the covers of a novel. Characters grow old with the reader, experience lust and loss, love and life. The thoughtfulness of his every detail and the concise placement of every word create a landscape more vivid than reality<br />One of the interesting topics of conversation in A Widow for One Year involves the main character’s attitude towards autobiographical fiction. Irving’s protagonist, world-famous author Ruth Cole, gives one hope that the powers of the imagination can compensate for lacking experience. Or, as would seem the case with my imagination, compensate for inobservance. When Ruth doubts her imagination and seeks real-life research for inspiration, she finds that reality is rarely as scripted and contained as her fiction. Appropriately enough, even her harsh reality is just a construct of Irving (although I am not familiar enough with him to know if it comes from his imagination or not). Ruth vehemently criticizes a journalistic tendency to mirror one’s stories on autobiography; yet one wonders how many of Irving’s repeat themes stick with him due to his personal history.<br /> <br />One aspect of A Widow for One Year that struck a chord (other than the glorious title) was the fact that, except for on a few rare occasions, the characters did not change. A love-struck boy, aged into his fifties, carries his idealized boyhood obsession throughout his adulthood. A philandering husband merely morphs into a philandering divorcé, and a woman bearing the sadness of the world carries it with her throughout self-inflicted exile. A whorish best friend does not let life jade her into changing, although she may at times feel a twinge of regret for the innocent pleasures she forfeits. In the end, who has changed? The characters have perhaps learned to understand each other, but doesn’t the understanding they receive allow them to sink ever deeper into the comfort of their own well-established personalities?<br />I also had the pleasure of watching the movie based on the beginning third of this book, entitled A Door in the Floor (starring Kim Basinger and Jeff Bridges). I thought it wise to focus solely on the exposition (if it could even be given such a flippant categorization), which in itself contained more that is real than your typical reader’s life. Although I would eventually become quite fond of our 30-something female protagonist who does not appear until the latter two-thirds, the episodes surrounding her during that fateful childhood summer are certainly what establish the story’s weight and depth of feeling. Ruth’s ability to create such powerful memories of family members that have only ever existed in photographs is a testament to the power of the imagination – both Ruth’s and John Irving’s.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10131275221021914336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10312259695691588416" style="display:none">Never have I wanted a house in the Hamptons more than after reading this book--okay, fine, I might have said that after watching <i>Grey Gardens</i> as well. <div id="review_3354550988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3354550988" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3354550988?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345432834" href="/book/show/44091121-widow-for-one-year">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3354550988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer897324139059271798">Once upon a time, I tried reading all of John Irving’s novels in a single year. It’s the first of my challenges to read an entire author’s catalog in chronological order that I failed. I’d successfully read all of Stephen King’s and (less impressively) Chuck Palaniuk’s books in a year and thought Irving would be a cakewalk. Boy, was I wrong. Irving’s books are far too similar for me to have completed the project. By the time I’d reached SON OF THE CIRCUS I was utterly burned out. I do wonder if </span>
  <span id="freeText897324139059271798" style="display:none">Once upon a time, I tried reading all of John Irving’s novels in a single year. It’s the first of my challenges to read an entire author’s catalog in chronological order that I failed. I’d successfully read all of Stephen King’s and (less impressively) Chuck Palaniuk’s books in a year and thought Irving would be a cakewalk. Boy, was I wrong. Irving’s books are far too similar for me to have completed the project. By the time I’d reached SON OF THE CIRCUS I was utterly burned out. I do wonder if I’d have liked the book more if I’d not picked it up directly after A PRAYER FOR OWEN MEANY… Anyway, I started A WIDOW FOR ONE YEAR three times before successfully reading it this fourth time. I’m glad I did. It is now my third favorite Irving novel, after only OWEN MEANY in second place, and THE WORLD ACCORDING TO GARP in the top spot. <br /><br />I find it hilarious that, in my Goodreads feed for this book, the top two reviews, both from women, say this:<br /><br />Example A: Irving is incapable of writing female characters.<br /><br />Example B: The only good part of the book is how REAL his characters are!<br /><br />How can that be? How can one woman say he is incapable, while the other says the characters are the best part of the novel? Well, simple. There are, as I see it, seven billion types of people on this planet, and women are individual people. I feel it’s incredibly solipsistic for anyone to assume that just because they don’t connect with a character that the author is incapable of connecting with others. It’s not like Irving had Ruth Cole boucing boobily down the stairs, or other such nonsense. There is quite a lot of talk about breasts, sure, but only as they relate to how men perceive Ruth.<br /> <br />I also feel that the characters in this book are far from the best part. They are terrific, don’t get me wrong, but what shines here is the writing. The book is impeccably written, and cyclical in nature. Everything comes full circle. This is truly a masterclass of character writing, theme, and omniscient perspective. <br /><br />Irving has a habit of meandering and droning seemingly without purpose. Then you reach a single sentence somewhere at the tail-end of the novel and everything that came before it falls into place. A single sentence: “Time doesn’t stop.” That is the pivot point on which this novel balances. Out of context, “Time doesn’t stop” is a simple truth. Time waits for no one. Tragedy befalls us all at some point and you must simply keep going. (The driving lessons in this book were the perfect delivery system for this lesson; just absolutely brilliant storytelling.) <br /><br />Yet in context of the novel, “Time doesn’t stop” has poignant and cathartic weight. The sentence hits like a fist. Then you get to the final few pages (Irving did this with SON OF THE CIRCUS, too; the final page was the best part of an otherwise disaster of a reading experience) and Irving punches you again with the reverse of “Time doesn’t stop” by showing you a moment in which time stands still. The more literary minded might see the final page as a bit of time travel. When I finished the book, I immediately reread the first chapter again, and that sealed the deal. What an absolutely fantastic, enthralling, complete experience this book was. <br /><br />I suppose if you read at surface level, this book could be a chore, so if you’re in it for plot, probably skip it. But if you’re looking for a beautiful story well told, look no further. <br /><br />Highly recommended. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="897324139059271798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_113635526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113635526" />
    <a title="Tocotin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4054892-tocotin"><img alt="Tocotin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280037257p2/4054892.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

  <span id="freeText474864040445174640" style="display:none">Just started... I don't know but what's with all the <i>italicized</i> words? Does the author do <i>this</i> in all his <i>books</i>?<br /><br />Omg I just finished it. It sucked so much. The characters were all flat, reduced to one quirk and one obsession, with maybe one exception (Rooie), and OMG again, why would a writer write about a writer writing about a writer? Really. :)</span>

<div id="review_5826459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5826459" />
    <a title="Eli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/162638-eli"><img alt="Eli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199836736p2/162638.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5826459?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eli" href="/user/show/162638-eli">Eli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/162638-eli?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5826459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2453099862266550363">The first thing that struck me about this book was the heart-stopping beauty of Marion, a central character near the beginning of the book. It's tough to get images that concrete in written words, but Irving handles it without strain. Its not just a physical description, its the way that the rest of the image is a bit darker, a bit fuzzier when Marion is in the picture, like Irving is using the depth of field in a photograph to highlight the subject, like her physical brilliance is so overwhelmi</span>
  <span id="freeText2453099862266550363" style="display:none">The first thing that struck me about this book was the heart-stopping beauty of Marion, a central character near the beginning of the book. It's tough to get images that concrete in written words, but Irving handles it without strain. Its not just a physical description, its the way that the rest of the image is a bit darker, a bit fuzzier when Marion is in the picture, like Irving is using the depth of field in a photograph to highlight the subject, like her physical brilliance is so overwhelming that everything else is dimmed.<br /><br />Looking back, though, that's really all that there was for me in this book. The post-Marion sections of the book are slushy and incongruous; where Irving was so careful not to create a caricature of beauty in the first part of the book, the later parts are almost nothing but caricature: The detective, the prostitute, the distant, womanizing father.<br /><br />In all, this was object lesson in leaving well enough alone. Had the first third of the book been left to stand on its own, it would have been a sparse, elegant novel. With the weight of the rest of the book, it was much less.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2453099862266550363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Adult Ruth was insufferable. Hannah was about four billion times more insufferable. Ruth's journal and novel excerpts-- yep, insufferab</span>
  <span id="freeText10221370754577858282" style="display:none">I hated about 89% of this book. The first part-- the whole 1958 part-- I really loved. Loved Eddie's goofy dad, the clam truck driver, Mrs. Vaughan, Ted drunkenly making Ruth grilled cheese. I was really excited to keep reading. <br /><br />I even loved the beginning of the next part-- Eddie running around in the rain trying to get to the book reading. After that? JUST WTF. <div id="review_2142501712" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2142501712" />
    <a title="J.K. Grice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72137025-j-k-grice"><img alt="J.K. Grice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507324623p2/72137025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2142501712?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.K. Grice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.K. Grice" href="/user/show/72137025-j-k-grice">J.K. Grice</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_271137884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271137884" />
    <a title="Bridgette Redman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7011555-bridgette-redman"><img alt="Bridgette Redman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328158182p2/7011555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271137884?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bridgette Redman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bridgette Redman" href="/user/show/7011555-bridgette-redman">Bridgette Redman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271137884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10195975640177032729">I’d forgotten what an intoxicating writer John Irving is. His compelling prose has a clarity and starkness that manages to entertain your brain and soul while permanently incorporating his characters and stories into your memory and being.<br/><br/>Irving is not one of those writers who kicks out a new novel every year. His novels are too carefully crafted, too (dare I say it?) literary to be anything less than an evolutionary process. After reading A Widow for One Year, I suspect his books are touchstone</span>
  <span id="freeText10195975640177032729" style="display:none">I’d forgotten what an intoxicating writer John Irving is. His compelling prose has a clarity and starkness that manages to entertain your brain and soul while permanently incorporating his characters and stories into your memory and being.<br /><br />Irving is not one of those writers who kicks out a new novel every year. His novels are too carefully crafted, too (dare I say it?) literary to be anything less than an evolutionary process. After reading A Widow for One Year, I suspect his books are touchstones in his life, each representing a period in which he explores an idea or a philosophy. <br /><br />Three in One<br /><br />Irving divides A Widow for One Year into three major sections. Don’t be misled though. None of the three are meant to stand on their own and each would be meaningless without the others.<br /><br />Book one takes place in the Hamptons in 1958. It is in this book that the events which will forever mold all of the characters take place. Irving also includes enough foreshadowing to clue us in on what will take place in the next two books. Somehow though, the spoilers he gives us in the first chapter do nothing but enhance the reading experience and when the events unravel, they still manage to be fresh and surprising.<br /><br />In this first book, Eddie falls irrevocably in love with Marion, Ruth learns to live with death and abandonment, Marion shuts down her heart, and Ted shows himself unable to change despite the traumatic events swirling around him.<br /><br />In book two, Ruth and Eddie meet as adults in 1990. We meet Hannah, Ruth’s best friend and other interesting characters. Irving takes us on a book publicity tour to Amsterdam and forces us to witness that which we would otherwise avoid.<br /><br />Book three takes place five years later (in 1995) and is a book of resolutions. Irving wraps up everyone’s plot lines very neatly. He very nearly gives us a “happily ever after” ending for each person. Happy ending or no, there is definitely an ending with no strings left to unravel.<br /><br />Characters<br /><br />Perhaps one of the most amazing things about Irving’s writing is his intense characterizations. There are no perfect heroes in Widow for One Year and very few villains. Even those people with whom we have the greatest exasperation show themselves in some aspect to be sympathetic. <br /><br />Irving’s characters are filled with quirks. They defy any sort of “norm” or stereotype. Indeed, perhaps some of the strength of Irving’s writing is that just when he’s gotten you to believe that a character is a stereotype, he shows you a different side of them or makes them act in a way that is unexpected, yet consistent with the character. <br /><br />Even the dead have a role in this novel. Marion and Ted’s two sons die four years before the novel begins yet they have a presence that is more than ghostly that permeates every page.<br /><br />Techniques<br /><br />Irving uses foreshadowing better than any other author I’ve ever read does. He tells you in first chapter how the book will end, yet no one will want to leave before he finishes telling the tale. Indeed, you’ll hang on every page to figure out how he will get to the ending he has foretold.<br /><br />Irving also frees his writing from the shackles of chronological time. For all that each book is “set” in a particular year, he freely moves back and forth using both character memories and foreshadowing, making the actual “when” almost irrelevant. A Widow for One Year is a nearly seamless picture of a lifetime. It doesn’t necessarily cover from birth to death, but you do feel you know everything you need to know about each person.<br /><br />One of the real treats in this novel are the stories-within-the story. Nearly all of the main characters are in the publishing industry, primarily as writers. Irving includes their writings as an integral part of the novel. He includes the complete text of two of Ted’s children’s books and summarizes the plots and themes of the novels of Ruth, Eddie, and Marion. We even get a slight peek at Hannah’s writings.<br /><br />A Writer’s Life<br /><br />A Widow for One Year is a book that absolutely resists being summarized in a banal statement such as “This book is about writing.” Or “This book is about sex.” The book is about many things, and is complex enough to have different meanings for different people. However, the complexity of the plot is not reflected in complex writing. It is a very easy book to read and nearly impossible to put down once you’ve started it.<br /><br />Having made that disclaimer, let me say that yet another delightful part of this book is the comments Irving makes on writers and writing. Irving tells us that writers are creative—they create what they write, and yet, even the most original writer draws on his or her experiences and knowledge. I can’t help but wonder whether some of the book tour scenes, interviews, and articles weren’t drawn from his own experience. At the very least, I think he enjoyed poking fun at some of the publishing industry’s foibles.<br /><br />Irving’s books have long ranked as some of my favorite novels. The World According to Garp is a must-read, and A Prayer for Owen Meany affected me like no other fiction book I’d ever read. While A Widow for One Year does not surpass A Prayer for Owen Meany, it comes very close.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10195975640177032729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_74699229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74699229" />
    <a title="Chana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1400289-chana"><img alt="Chana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373441206p2/1400289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74699229?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chana" href="/user/show/1400289-chana">Chana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345424716" href="/book/show/386330.A_Widow_for_One_Year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=not-for-kids">not-for-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=fiction-family-drama">fiction-family-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=literature-fiction">literature-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74699229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5088971722429211741">I had really expected something different. This is the 1st of his books that I have read but I knew he wrote The World According to Garp, Cider House Rules and A Prayer for Owen Meany. Maybe it is just this book but I have to say that Mr. Irving has his mind in the gutter. (sorry to all of you who think this was a terrible thing to say!) He is funny sometimes and he does write memorable scenes, however... right now we are perusing the red light district in Amsterdam. There is sex on just about e</span>
  <span id="freeText5088971722429211741" style="display:none">I had really expected something different. This is the 1st of his books that I have read but I knew he wrote The World According to Garp, Cider House Rules and A Prayer for Owen Meany. Maybe it is just this book but I have to say that Mr. Irving has his mind in the gutter. (sorry to all of you who think this was a terrible thing to say!) He is funny sometimes and he does write memorable scenes, however... right now we are perusing the red light district in Amsterdam. There is sex on just about every page of this book and I am sick of it! (more than 1/2 way through the book)<br /><br />OK, I am finished now. He can write a scene (the shredded pornographic drawings in the hedge, the gardner stuck in the hedge - I mean this is memorable writing. He is often funny (his commentary about the use of the semicolon in modern writing had me laughing out loud). The children's stories are terrifying. The Moleman is definitely nightmare material. His writing is compared to Dickens and rightly so, I mean it IS that good. However, and I do mean a BIG however, there are some things I really object to here.<br /><br />#1 A bereaved mother has an affair with a teenage boy. She is susceptible to this because she lost her teenage sons?? As a bereaved mother I find this kind of thinking just incomprehensible. I also didn’t think leaving Ruth was realistic. Most bereaved mothers cling to their surviving children. <br /><br />#2 There is so much sex in this book. It is degrading, and feels insulting. A quote from the book:<br /><br />"But grossness was the norm for many people. Crudeness and prurient interests were the motivating humors for all sorts of individuals;...Whereas she wished more of the population were better educated, she also believed that education was largely wasted on the majority of people she had met."<br />(Mrs. Dash - a character in one of Ruth's stories)<br /><br />another quote:<br /><br />"It galls me that seeking out the seedy, the sordid, the sexual, and the deviant is the expected (if not altogether acceptable) behavior of male writers; it would surely benefit me, as a writer, if I had the courage to seek out more of the seedy, the sordid, the sexual, and the deviant myself."<br />(Ruth, the main female character, a writer) <br /><br />#3 His obsession with breasts makes this story about Ruth's breasts as much as about anything else. Do you think I exaggerate? By the end I think the author must have been joking, the references became so frequent: <br /><br /><br />page 448: <br />"In Ruth's case, you couldn't even see her breasts."<br />page 449:<br />"Yeah, she had nice breasts."<br />page 451:<br />"She had nice breasts, Harry remembered."<br />page 478:<br />"It may have been his anniversary, but he was looking at your breasts."<br />page 482:<br />"And she really did have great breasts."<br />page 486:<br />"Graham won't leave my breasts alone."<br /><br />This is just a tiny sampling of the constant reference to Ruth's breasts. And there are Marion's breasts, Mrs. Havelock's breasts, ... ad naseaum.<br /><br />These aren't the only body parts we hear about, but I've had enough.<br /><br />He also throws the character of Ted away. Ruth has "no feelings" about this. I seem to have more feelings about it than Ruth does! Ruth was the only person Ted ever loved in his life. Was her "no feelings" supposed to be his just reward for how he treated other women? <br /><br />Editing this review to say I recommend reading him, despite my criticism. I've read five of his books and will read the rest as I find them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5088971722429211741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="devon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="devon" href="/user/show/32090-devon">devon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2526930&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2526930" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13315640732212535615">I don't much know how I feel about this one. The first section of the book is completely brilliant. As is the last line. But in between is rushed, contrite, and full of coincidences that seems like cheap ways to move the story along. Irving gets around conflict in the second part of the book by killing people off. Don't want to deal with the Ruth/Ted conflict? Kill him off! Don't want ruth to have to face her husband about what happened in Amsterdam? Kill him off! And apparently, having a baby s</span>
  <span id="freeText13315640732212535615" style="display:none">I don't much know how I feel about this one. The first section of the book is completely brilliant. As is the last line. But in between is rushed, contrite, and full of coincidences that seems like cheap ways to move the story along. Irving gets around conflict in the second part of the book by killing people off. Don't want to deal with the Ruth/Ted conflict? Kill him off! Don't want ruth to have to face her husband about what happened in Amsterdam? Kill him off! And apparently, having a baby solves all internal conflict. (Didn't we learn at the beginning of the book how untrue that is? Shouldn't Graham's resemblance to Timothy have some affect on Ruth?) It's particularly frustrating when a book that takes such care to develop its characters and relationships throws them into over the top plot devices and removes their complexity of emotions. The description of Ruth's feelings about widowhood are quickly jumped over and given half-ass descriptions when contrasted with the loving detail given to Eddie's desire, Marion's sorrow, and even the hilarity of Ted's mistress/gardner scene (which is wickedly funny). I keep trying to justify it by thinking that perhaps the different parts are supposed to be reflective of the novelists in the story, the careful character development of Ruth's style, the crime caper of Marion's style. In the end, though, it feels more like an Eddie O'Hare, deeply and painfully realistic when it sticks to the good stuff, but ultimately fake-feeling, forced, and unsatisfying.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13315640732212535615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/849321-jeana?shelf=good-fiction">good-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer23160903&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer23160903" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14599649129977132042">I was very close to not finishing this book around page 350 (the first section wasn't so bad, but the middle really lagged). It was very long (very wordy) and to be honest, I didn't care much for Ruth or Eddie. <br/><br/>I am glad, however, that I continued reading because it got much better toward the end. I started liking Ruth only when she got married and became a mother. It changed something in her, I suppose. <br/><br/>Although this was not one of my favorite books by far, I did like the way John Irving could </span>
  <span id="freeText14599649129977132042" style="display:none">I was very close to not finishing this book around page 350 (the first section wasn't so bad, but the middle really lagged). It was very long (very wordy) and to be honest, I didn't care much for Ruth or Eddie. <br /><br />I am glad, however, that I continued reading because it got much better toward the end. I started liking Ruth only when she got married and became a mother. It changed something in her, I suppose. <br /><br />Although this was not one of my favorite books by far, I did like the way John Irving could take something from the beginning of the story--such as Ruth as a child being afraid of a dress moving on a hanger, which I believe came from one of her father's childrens' books, and brought that back when she was waiting in the closet of the red room in Amsterdam. Just having witnessed a murder. His ability to bring back elements like that was nice. And he did it more than once.<br /><br />This book, though, was exhausting in its amount of sex and all things related. And enough about how Ruth had great breasts. I get it. I got it after the first time they mentioned it. <br /><br />Anyway, I don't know that I would recommend this book to anyone I know (in fact, I know I wouldn't) but the last 200 pages redeemed itself for me. But the ending tied itself up a little too neatly for me. </span>

<div id="review_23251914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23251914" />
    <a title="Erick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/184356-erick"><img alt="Erick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443213693p2/184356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23251914?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erick" href="/user/show/184356-erick">Erick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23251914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16493551371152507766">The first couple hundred pages of the book, before it jumps forward several decades, are the most even, and it is this part of the story that is most endearing. This first part introduces us to the story's three or four main characters and chronicles their shared summer of 1958--a summer which, you guessed it, has profound effects on the rest of all their lives.<br/><br/>And it is much of the rest of these lives that Irving takes us through in the remaining four hundred pages, and due to the front-heavy n</span>
  <span id="freeText16493551371152507766" style="display:none">The first couple hundred pages of the book, before it jumps forward several decades, are the most even, and it is this part of the story that is most endearing. This first part introduces us to the story's three or four main characters and chronicles their shared summer of 1958--a summer which, you guessed it, has profound effects on the rest of all their lives.<br /><br />And it is much of the rest of these lives that Irving takes us through in the remaining four hundred pages, and due to the front-heavy nature of the book's dramatic set-up it's probably unavoidable that the follow-through seems noticeably lacking in some intensity.<br /><br />But I was still happy to follow the characters along, and for the most part they all seem to end up in their correct places. I didn't buy every plot turn/twist (and the shaky pages set in Amsterdam make it clear that Irving is far more at home in his native New England), but Irving is a great storyteller and easily convinces the reader to weather the impressive storm of plot mechanics he (again) rouses to move things along. All in all: An enjoyable read, but pick up the more-satisfying <i>Prayer for Owen Meany</i> first.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16493551371152507766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14841966883413113148">My first Irving and it did not disappoint! </span>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3261745803">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_417892209" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417892209" />
    <a title="Mitch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2946068-mitch"><img alt="Mitch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570799984p2/2946068.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417892209?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mitch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mitch" href="/user/show/2946068-mitch">Mitch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2946068-mitch?shelf=just-fiction">just-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417892209" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12628165966272676391">This is one of those 537-page books that, after you've read about 100 pages, you realize you've pretty much seen what the author's handing out and you don't care for it. This is a drag because there is so much more of it to go and only some misguided impulse to finish what you've started goads you stubbornly onward.<br/><br/>What's not to like? Well, here's some: <br/><br/>At times Irving writes with the grace of a ballet dancer and at others he lumbers along like a blindfolded football player looking for an exit. </span>
  <span id="freeText12628165966272676391" style="display:none">This is one of those 537-page books that, after you've read about 100 pages, you realize you've pretty much seen what the author's handing out and you don't care for it. This is a drag because there is so much more of it to go and only some misguided impulse to finish what you've started goads you stubbornly onward.<br /><br />What's not to like? Well, here's some: <br /><br />At times Irving writes with the grace of a ballet dancer and at others he lumbers along like a blindfolded football player looking for an exit. The word 'clumsy' comes to mind.<br /><br />I found some of the things his characters said, and many of their actions, unlikely and unbelievable. (His main character chooses to build her workroom in the room where her father committed suicide after a fight with her. She can still smell carbon monoxide fumes (his chosen exit strategy) from the cars below. How...atmospheric. This is a good place to write a novel?)<br /><br />The characters' interactions and lives also seem unreal. The knotted combinations of who is in love or having sex with whom, the main character finding lasting love after a couple of weeks of being with a guy after her continual failures with other men...just no, okay? <br /><br />Also objectionable: the 'comic' parts that deteriorate into vaudeville slapstick...specifically the jilted adulteress gunning for her seducer with her car. <br /><br />One thing that was interesting was a convoluted device that had characters commenting and reflecting on how much or little novel writing contains autobiographical vs. imaginative content. At times this was like looking in a mirror and seeing yourself holding a mirror and looking back. <br /><br />Even so, I will unhesitatingly jilt John himself. I hope he doesn't have a Lincoln!<br /><br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12628165966272676391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2345102197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2345102197" />
    <a title="Sharon Metcalf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49089020-sharon-metcalf"><img alt="Sharon Metcalf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451815807p2/49089020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2345102197?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharon Metcalf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharon Metcalf" href="/user/show/49089020-sharon-metcalf">Sharon Metcalf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552146685" href="/book/show/895395.A_Widow_for_One_Year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49089020-sharon-metcalf?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49089020-sharon-metcalf?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2345102197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12132250680165092376">John Irvings website describes A Widow For One Year as <i>" Richly comic, as well as deeply disturbing, A Widow for One Year is a multilayered love story of astonishing emotional force. Both ribald and erotic, it is also a brilliant novel about the passage of time and the relentlessness of grief." </i> It all sounds so appealing to me so what a shame it is to discover that after reading all 668 pages my <i>astonishing emotional responses</i> were relief that I can now move on to something else, and disappoint</span>
  <span id="freeText12132250680165092376" style="display:none">John Irvings website describes A Widow For One Year as <i>" Richly comic, as well as deeply disturbing, A Widow for One Year is a multilayered love story of astonishing emotional force. Both ribald and erotic, it is also a brilliant novel about the passage of time and the relentlessness of grief." </i> It all sounds so appealing to me so what a shame it is to discover that after reading all 668 pages my <i>astonishing emotional responses</i> were relief that I can now move on to something else, and disappointment that I clearly missed alot of what was great about it. <br /><br />From early on I got the sense that I wasn't going to be enthralled by this book so should have let it go yet I have this ridiculous stubborn streak that prevents me from giving up once I've started. Spanning almost four decades it was something of a family saga yet I felt apathetic about most of the characters. It had a tongue-in-cheek feel about it yet what was referred to as ribald I didn't really find funny, (although I will admit the very last line in the book was cleverly done and made me smile). I could recognise the love story and the elements of grief but simply didn't feel them as I expected to. <br /><br />Whilst I suspect I'll forget the plot and the characters in no time I fear the disappointment will linger longer. John Irving is a highly regarded author and I own many of his most famous titles. I'm sure I'll try him again but it might just take me a while to want to go there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12132250680165092376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="E" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/472694-e"><img alt="E" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517657085p2/472694.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/359660953?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="E" class="user" itemprop="url" name="E" href="/user/show/472694-e">E</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345434791" href="/book/show/228097.A_Widow_for_One_Year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/472694-e?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer359660953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3376038468634707841">The first half is Irving at his best. All those hooks on the wall. All that broken glass and all those windblown sketches. All that honesty amid all that posturing. All that clunky sex amid endless guilt.<br/><br/>The second half embodies the detachment, the absurdity, the too-many-ideas-at-once that the main characters have grown to embrace. Perhaps they are defense mechanisms. Perhaps they are the inevitable scars of emotional abuse. Marion is so matter-of-fact about her fate, so resigned to a life that</span>
  <span id="freeText3376038468634707841" style="display:none">The first half is Irving at his best. All those hooks on the wall. All that broken glass and all those windblown sketches. All that honesty amid all that posturing. All that clunky sex amid endless guilt.<br /><br />The second half embodies the detachment, the absurdity, the too-many-ideas-at-once that the main characters have grown to embrace. Perhaps they are defense mechanisms. Perhaps they are the inevitable scars of emotional abuse. Marion is so matter-of-fact about her fate, so resigned to a life that will never bring closure, and she drags everyone along with her toward this anti-conclusion. It's unsettling. But what could we expect? Is widowhood, is the death of one's children, is witnessing a murder supposed to be anything <i>besides</i> unsettling?<br /><br />The story is frustrating, but I'm overcome with the sensation that Irving knew exactly what he was doing all along. Frustration is a bold goal to set for oneself, but I can't imagine Irving any other way but bold. It's as ingrained in his writing as the New England landscape. <br /><br />And the novel's jarring last line rivals <i>Angela's Ashes</i> for Most Impressive Last Line Ever. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3376038468634707841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2986137862" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2986137862" />
    <a title="DebsD" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59633249-debsd"><img alt="DebsD" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485019761p2/59633249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2986137862?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DebsD" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DebsD" href="/user/show/59633249-debsd">DebsD</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552997966" href="/book/show/816626.A_Widow_For_One_Year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=rating-4-stars-and-a-bit">rating-4-stars-and-a-bit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=family-home">family-home</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=ratings-4s-5s-plus">ratings-4s-5s-plus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59633249-debsd?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2986137862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2914270444029411277">I picked this up because I've enjoyed several of Irving's other novels; having read it, I'm looking forward to my next one. This is a long novel and not especially fast-paced, but I was engaged from the start and one of my favourite moments occurs right at the end - I was never disinterested.<br/><br/>Irving is very much a story-teller. He carefully builds the structure of his tales and it shows - but his characters are as strong as his stories, and neither suffers from the attention paid to the other. He</span>
  <span id="freeText2914270444029411277" style="display:none">I picked this up because I've enjoyed several of Irving's other novels; having read it, I'm looking forward to my next one. This is a long novel and not especially fast-paced, but I was engaged from the start and one of my favourite moments occurs right at the end - I was never disinterested.<br /><br />Irving is very much a story-teller. He carefully builds the structure of his tales and it shows - but his characters are as strong as his stories, and neither suffers from the attention paid to the other. He combines plot and character in a way that I think few authors do. His characters come to life; they are never boring - even minor characters are given detail and context that makes them memorable. Many of his characters could be ridiculous, but they aren't - instead they engage the reader's sympathy. I can see parts of people I know in many of his characters, and parts of many of his characters in people I know.<br /><br />I will continue to work my way through the Irving backlist!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2914270444029411277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1798602964" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1798602964" />
    <a title="Cody | CodysBookshelf" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf"><img alt="Cody | CodysBookshelf" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592500568p2/56352820.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1798602964?book_show_action=true">Apr 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cody | CodysBookshelf" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cody | CodysBookshelf" href="/user/show/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf">Cody | CodysBookshelf</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780676970807" href="/book/show/587402.A_Widow_for_One_Year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56352820-cody-codysbookshelf?shelf=john-irving">john-irving</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1798602964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12642243453063241653">“What greater thing is there for two human souls, than to feel that they are joined for life - to strengthen each other in all labor, to rest on each other in all sorrow, to minister to each other in all pain, to be one with each other in silent unspeakable memories at the moment of the last parting?“<br/><br/>Oh, John Irving. You have done it again. You have taken my heart and torn it in two; you have opened my eyes a little more. <br/><br/>Published in 1998, exactly twenty years after <i>The World According to Garp</i>,</span>
  <span id="freeText12642243453063241653" style="display:none">“What greater thing is there for two human souls, than to feel that they are joined for life - to strengthen each other in all labor, to rest on each other in all sorrow, to minister to each other in all pain, to be one with each other in silent unspeakable memories at the moment of the last parting?“<br /><br />Oh, John Irving. You have done it again. You have taken my heart and torn it in two; you have opened my eyes a little more. <br /><br />Published in 1998, exactly twenty years after <i>The World According to Garp</i>, <i>A Widow for One Year</i> is in many ways a spiritual successor to that modern classic. It deals with themes of loss; at the fore is not just one author but four; Irving again touches on themes like lost parents and prostitution and strange sexual situations. This is John Irving — you either like it, or you don’t. I certainly do.<br /><br />Unlike <i>Garp</i>, however, underlying this tome is a theme of redemption, hope. If <i>Garp</i> is stark winter — for it is quite bleak, pessimistic — then <i>Widow</i> is early Autumn: this story is told in shades of light brown and orange. <br /><br />If <i>A Prayer for Owen Meany</i> did not exist, this would be my favorite Irving novel. A beautiful rumination on long loves and the creative process, this is worthy reading for anyone who appreciates a well-rendered literary journey. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12642243453063241653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3314345458" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3314345458" />
    <a title="Richard Seltzer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110610415-richard-seltzer"><img alt="Richard Seltzer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588366231p2/110610415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3314345458?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Seltzer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Seltzer" href="/user/show/110610415-richard-seltzer">Richard Seltzer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3314345458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11443867802183881088">This book repeatedly asks the question "what is fiction about?" Is the source of characters and events personal experience or imagination? Four main characters are authors (Ruth, Eddie, Marion, and Ted). We see how their experiences and their fictional transformations of those experiences are intertwined, with stories inside stories. The stories these characters tell and write are important in moving the main plot along, e.g., Marion's mystery novels, Ruth's novel with the scene in Amsterdam, Te</span>
  <span id="freeText11443867802183881088" style="display:none">This book repeatedly asks the question "what is fiction about?" Is the source of characters and events personal experience or imagination? Four main characters are authors (Ruth, Eddie, Marion, and Ted). We see how their experiences and their fictional transformations of those experiences are intertwined, with stories inside stories. The stories these characters tell and write are important in moving the main plot along, e.g., Marion's mystery novels, Ruth's novel with the scene in Amsterdam, Ted's use of his children's story writer reputation to seduce young mothers. Sometimes the retelling of an event as an event itself (for instance, telling about the Tommy/Timmy accident) and it is a new event for each person it is told to. (The telling of a story is an event in a story, as is telling about that telling of a story...) Also, two other important characters are avid readers (Minty and Harry) whose lives are deeply affected by what they read.<br /><br />The property on Long Island also plays an important role in the story. Its changing physical layout becomes intertwined with the characters and the action. For instance: the photos of Timmy and Tommy on the walls, then just picture hanger hooks, then new photos of Ruth, then the photos gone and the holes filled in and painted over; first the back yard is rough and wild and untended, then when Marion leaves, Ted adds the swimming pool, shower and privet hedge; the squash court becomes a suicide scene, then an office, and will probably be used in new ways as Marion moves back at the end). In other words, the property is both a physical given and a human construct. The layout leads to events that affect people's lives (the memories of pictures and picture hangers; the four-year-old Ruth wandering into the master bedroom and seeing her mother with Eddie); and their experiences lead them to change the layout.<br /><br />The characters and the incidents are consistently engaging. There's anticipation and surprise, humor and pathos from start to finish. This is Irving's most consistently brilliant and well-crafted novel. It is also a book about the craft/life of writing fiction. This is the mature work of an excellent writer -- one who began with great talent and promise and now writes with mastery.<br /><br />The stories in his previous books centered around humorously improbable events and grotesquely exaggerated characters -- like the conception of Garp and the bear in Hotel New Hampshire. Widow feels real and immediate. Here Irving seems to extract more meaning from the events he recounts, rather than rushing on to tell of more events. Here he creates a fully wrought story, rather than a series of uneven but often brilliant sketches.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11443867802183881088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3042885723" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3800729118505370089">In my late teens and early twenties I used to read Irving's books all the time, I think I must've read "a widow for one year" at least 4 or 5 times - it was one of my favourite books of his. I'd recently decided to read it again (it's been years...), this time in English. It's not the book I used to love. Or maybe I'm not the person I used to be. It's been a surreal experience. The story, the characters, the language, nothing was like it used to be. It was like reading two different books on the</span>
  <span id="freeText3800729118505370089" style="display:none">In my late teens and early twenties I used to read Irving's books all the time, I think I must've read "a widow for one year" at least 4 or 5 times - it was one of my favourite books of his. I'd recently decided to read it again (it's been years...), this time in English. It's not the book I used to love. Or maybe I'm not the person I used to be. It's been a surreal experience. The story, the characters, the language, nothing was like it used to be. It was like reading two different books on the same subjects... My younger me used to give ***** to this book without hesitation. Now I would've given it ***. So 4 stars it is. (Part of me wonders if I used to love this book BECAUSE of its Polish translation, hence my disappointment with the original, or whether I've outgrown his writing in general 🤔)</span>

<div id="review_1454391542" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1454391542" />
    <a title="Celia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30261451-celia"><img alt="Celia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466854747p2/30261451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1454391542?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celia" href="/user/show/30261451-celia">Celia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780739320914" href="/book/show/7104391-a-widow-for-one-year">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=booklender">booklender</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=well-read">well-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1454391542" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14751010307859588212">Another winner from John Irving.<br/><br/>I listened to A Widow for One Year on Audio CD read by one of my favorite narrators, George Guidall.<br/><br/>It is a book about authors and their books and their lives. It spans 37 years in the lives of Ruth Cole and Eddie O'Hare.<br/><br/>Ruth is the protagonist. Her parents are Ted and Marion Cole. Eddie is hired by Ted to be his driver one summer (Ted is an alcoholic who has had his driver's license suspended). Eddie becomes one of the family.<br/><br/>This book is Irving's ninth novel, an</span>
  <span id="freeText14751010307859588212" style="display:none">Another winner from John Irving.<br /><br />I listened to A Widow for One Year on Audio CD read by one of my favorite narrators, George Guidall.<br /><br />It is a book about authors and their books and their lives. It spans 37 years in the lives of Ruth Cole and Eddie O'Hare.<br /><br />Ruth is the protagonist. Her parents are Ted and Marion Cole. Eddie is hired by Ted to be his driver one summer (Ted is an alcoholic who has had his driver's license suspended). Eddie becomes one of the family.<br /><br />This book is Irving's ninth novel, and, according to some, very intricately crafted. I did enjoy the story and its unfolding and its end. Some of the characters were to be disliked, but I loved Ruth, so putting up with the others was just part of the bargain.<br /><br />At 500+ pages, it was still a quick read because it flowed so effortlessly.<br /><br />4 stars<br /><br />PS. Please let me know what YOUR favorite Irving book is. I have read A Prayer for Owen Meany and LOVED it. Probably no other Irving book as 'Owen' will be so beloved by me, but I am going to read at least 3 more of his books. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14751010307859588212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_432229737" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/432229737" />
    <a title="Irene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9077572-irene"><img alt="Irene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402352450p2/9077572.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/432229737?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irene" href="/user/show/9077572-irene">Irene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer432229737" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18098679522653806134">This is the only Irving novel I've read so far, but I plan to read more in the future. I read "A widow for one year" about a year ago, and I picked it unknowingly in the bookshop without knowing anything about the author, his style or the story. To be honest, I picked it up just because:<br/><br/>a) It was long and it was summer<br/>b) It began with the sentence "One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking -it was coming from her parent</span>
  <span id="freeText18098679522653806134" style="display:none">This is the only Irving novel I've read so far, but I plan to read more in the future. I read "A widow for one year" about a year ago, and I picked it unknowingly in the bookshop without knowing anything about the author, his style or the story. To be honest, I picked it up just because:<br /><br />a) It was long and it was summer<br />b) It began with the sentence "One night when she was four and sleeping in the bottom bunk of her bunk bed, Ruth Cole woke to the sound of lovemaking -it was coming from her parents' bedroom".<br /><br />You can beat a beginning like that one, but it's gonna be hard.<br /><br />So I did what I'm not used to do: I entered a literary work without any clues whatsoever. It was great.<br /><br />John Irving is a story-teller, in the widest sense. He takes a character and builds a story around him, normally adding loads of special characters to accompany him. He tells the story with slow, careful writing, paying the right attention to details (the brother's stories and how Ruth memorises the stories through the photographs, just genious) and keeping the reader interested. His stories are long, and he might get a bit tough from time to time, but it's worth it. What I loved the most was how true, how honest, how real his characters were. Neither good nor bad, just people living hard situations and not always taking the right road.<br /><br />I preferred the first part of the book, which focuses on Ruth's parents relationship, rather than the second one (when Ruth is actually old enough to have her own relationships). That might have been because I liked the parents best, and I found both of them appealing and intriguing, I wanted to know what was wrong with them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18098679522653806134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
